<meta name="DC.Description" content="### Introduction

Darwin famously characterized the rapid rise and early diversification of flowering plants (angiosperms) in the fossil record as an “abominable mystery.” Identifying genomic changes that accompanied the origin of angiosperms is key to unraveling the molecular basis of biological innovations that contributed to their geologically near-instantaneous rise to ecological dominance.

![Graphic][1]&lt;/img&gt;

Amborella trichopoda , an understory shrub endemic to New Caledonia, is the sole surviving sister species of all other living flowering plants (angiosperms). The Amborella genome provides an exceptional reference for inferring features of the first flowering plants and identifies an ancient angiosperm-wide whole-genome duplication (red star). Amborella flowers have spirally arranged tepals, unfused carpels (female; shown), and laminar stamens. Amborella trichopoda , an understory shrub endemic to New Caledonia, is the sole surviving sister species of all other living flowering plants (angiosperms). The Amborella genome provides an exceptional reference for inferring features of the first flowering plants and identifies an ancient angiosperm-wide whole-genome duplication (red star). Amborella flowers have spirally arranged tepals, unfused carpels (female; shown), and laminar stamens.

### Methods

We provide a draft genome for Amborella trichopoda , the single living representative of the sister lineage to all other extant flowering plants and use phylogenomic and comparative genomic analyses to elucidate ancestral gene content and genome structure in the most recent common ancestor of all living angiosperms.

### Results

We reveal that an ancient genome duplication predated angiosperm diversification. However, unlike all other sequenced angiosperm genomes, the Amborella genome shows no evidence of more recent, lineage-specific genome duplications, making Amborella particularly well suited to help interpret genomic changes after polyploidy in other angiosperms. The remarkable conservation of gene order (synteny) among the genomes of Amborella and other angiosperms has enabled reconstruction of the ancestral gene arrangement in eudicots (~75% of all angiosperms). An ancestral angiosperm gene set was inferred to contain at least 14,000 protein-coding genes; subsequent changes in gene content and genome structure across disparate flowering plant lineages are associated with the evolution of important crops and model species. Relative to nonangiosperm seed plants, 1179 gene lineages first appeared in association with the origin of the angiosperms. These include genes important in flowering, wood formation, and responses to environmental stress. Unlike other angiosperms, the Amborella genome lacks evidence for recent transposon insertions while retaining ancient and divergent transposons. The genome harbors an abundance of atypical lineage-specific 24-nucleotide microRNAs, with at least 27 regulatory microRNA families inferred to have been present in the ancestral angiosperm. Population genomic analysis of 12 individuals from across the small native range of Amborella in New Caledonia reveals geographic structure with conservation implications, as well as both a recent genetic bottleneck and high levels of genome diversity.

### Discussion

The Amborella genome is a pivotal reference for understanding genome and gene family evolution throughout angiosperm history. Genome structure and phylogenomic analyses indicate that the ancestral angiosperm was a polyploid with a large constellation of both novel and ancient genes that survived to play key roles in angiosperm biology.

Amborella trichopoda is understood to be the most basal extant flowering plant and its genome is anticipated to provide insights into the evolution of plant life on Earth (see the Perspective by [Adams][2] ). To validate and assemble the sequence, Chamala et al. (p. [1516][3]) combined fluorescent in situ hybridization (FISH), genomic mapping, and next-generation sequencing. The Amborella Genome Project (p. [10.1126/science.1241089][4]) was able to infer that a whole-genome duplication event preceded the evolution of this ancestral angiosperm, and Rice et al. (p. [1468][5]) found that numerous genes in the mitochondrion were acquired by horizontal gene transfer from other plants, including almost four entire mitochondrial genomes from mosses and algae.

Amborella trichopoda is strongly supported as the single living species of the sister lineage to all other extant flowering plants, providing a unique reference for inferring the genome content and structure of the most recent common ancestor (MRCA) of living angiosperms. Sequencing the Amborella genome, we identified an ancient genome duplication predating angiosperm diversification, without evidence of subsequent, lineage-specific genome duplications. Comparisons between Amborella and other angiosperms facilitated reconstruction of the ancestral angiosperm gene content and gene order in the MRCA of core eudicots. We identify new gene families, gene duplications, and floral protein-protein interactions that first appeared in the ancestral angiosperm. Transposable elements in Amborella are ancient and highly divergent, with no recent transposon radiations. Population genomic analysis across Amborella ’s native range in New Caledonia reveals a recent genetic bottleneck and geographic structure with conservation implications.

&lt;p&gt;Darwin famously characterized the rapid rise and early diversification of flowering plants (angiosperms) in the fossil record as an “abominable mystery.” Identifying genomic changes that accompanied the origin of angiosperms is key to unraveling the molecular basis of biological innovations that contributed to their geologically near-instantaneous rise to ecological dominance.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;Amborella trichopoda&lt;/i&gt;, an understory shrub endemic to New Caledonia, is the sole surviving sister species of all other living flowering plants (angiosperms). However, unlike all other sequenced angiosperm genomes, the &lt;i&gt;Amborella &lt;/i&gt;genome shows no evidence of more recent, lineage-specific genome duplications, making &lt;i&gt;Amborella &lt;/i&gt;particularly well suited to help interpret genomic changes after polyploidy in other angiosperms. The genome harbors an abundance of atypical lineage-specific 24-nucleotide microRNAs, with at least 27 regulatory microRNA families inferred to have been present in the ancestral angiosperm. &lt;p&gt;The &lt;i&gt;Amborella&lt;/i&gt; genome is a pivotal reference for understanding genome and gene family evolution throughout angiosperm history. Genome structure and phylogenomic analyses indicate that the ancestral angiosperm was a polyploid with a large constellation of both novel and ancient genes that survived to play key roles in angiosperm biology.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="The Amborella Genome and the Evolution of Flowering Plants" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/342/6165/1241089/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="### Introduction

Darwin famously characterized the rapid rise and early diversification of flowering plants (angiosperms) in the fossil record as an “abominable mystery.” Identifying genomic changes that accompanied the origin of angiosperms is key to unraveling the molecular basis of biological innovations that contributed to their geologically near-instantaneous rise to ecological dominance.

![Graphic][1]&lt;/img&gt;

Amborella trichopoda , an understory shrub endemic to New Caledonia, is the sole surviving sister species of all other living flowering plants (angiosperms). The Amborella genome provides an exceptional reference for inferring features of the first flowering plants and identifies an ancient angiosperm-wide whole-genome duplication (red star). Amborella flowers have spirally arranged tepals, unfused carpels (female; shown), and laminar stamens. Amborella trichopoda , an understory shrub endemic to New Caledonia, is the sole surviving sister species of all other living flowering plants (angiosperms). The Amborella genome provides an exceptional reference for inferring features of the first flowering plants and identifies an ancient angiosperm-wide whole-genome duplication (red star). Amborella flowers have spirally arranged tepals, unfused carpels (female; shown), and laminar stamens.

### Methods

We provide a draft genome for Amborella trichopoda , the single living representative of the sister lineage to all other extant flowering plants and use phylogenomic and comparative genomic analyses to elucidate ancestral gene content and genome structure in the most recent common ancestor of all living angiosperms.

### Results

We reveal that an ancient genome duplication predated angiosperm diversification. However, unlike all other sequenced angiosperm genomes, the Amborella genome shows no evidence of more recent, lineage-specific genome duplications, making Amborella particularly well suited to help interpret genomic changes after polyploidy in other angiosperms. The remarkable conservation of gene order (synteny) among the genomes of Amborella and other angiosperms has enabled reconstruction of the ancestral gene arrangement in eudicots (~75% of all angiosperms). An ancestral angiosperm gene set was inferred to contain at least 14,000 protein-coding genes; subsequent changes in gene content and genome structure across disparate flowering plant lineages are associated with the evolution of important crops and model species. Relative to nonangiosperm seed plants, 1179 gene lineages first appeared in association with the origin of the angiosperms. These include genes important in flowering, wood formation, and responses to environmental stress. Unlike other angiosperms, the Amborella genome lacks evidence for recent transposon insertions while retaining ancient and divergent transposons. The genome harbors an abundance of atypical lineage-specific 24-nucleotide microRNAs, with at least 27 regulatory microRNA families inferred to have been present in the ancestral angiosperm. Population genomic analysis of 12 individuals from across the small native range of Amborella in New Caledonia reveals geographic structure with conservation implications, as well as both a recent genetic bottleneck and high levels of genome diversity.

### Discussion

The Amborella genome is a pivotal reference for understanding genome and gene family evolution throughout angiosperm history. Genome structure and phylogenomic analyses indicate that the ancestral angiosperm was a polyploid with a large constellation of both novel and ancient genes that survived to play key roles in angiosperm biology.

Amborella trichopoda is understood to be the most basal extant flowering plant and its genome is anticipated to provide insights into the evolution of plant life on Earth (see the Perspective by [Adams][2] ). To validate and assemble the sequence, Chamala et al. (p. [1516][3]) combined fluorescent in situ hybridization (FISH), genomic mapping, and next-generation sequencing. The Amborella Genome Project (p. [10.1126/science.1241089][4]) was able to infer that a whole-genome duplication event preceded the evolution of this ancestral angiosperm, and Rice et al. (p. [1468][5]) found that numerous genes in the mitochondrion were acquired by horizontal gene transfer from other plants, including almost four entire mitochondrial genomes from mosses and algae.

Amborella trichopoda is strongly supported as the single living species of the sister lineage to all other extant flowering plants, providing a unique reference for inferring the genome content and structure of the most recent common ancestor (MRCA) of living angiosperms. Sequencing the Amborella genome, we identified an ancient genome duplication predating angiosperm diversification, without evidence of subsequent, lineage-specific genome duplications. Comparisons between Amborella and other angiosperms facilitated reconstruction of the ancestral angiosperm gene content and gene order in the MRCA of core eudicots. We identify new gene families, gene duplications, and floral protein-protein interactions that first appeared in the ancestral angiosperm. Transposable elements in Amborella are ancient and highly divergent, with no recent transposon radiations. Population genomic analysis across Amborella ’s native range in New Caledonia reveals a recent genetic bottleneck and geographic structure with conservation implications.

    <script id="loadcss">
      /*! CSS rel=preload polyfill. Depends on loadCSS function. [c]2016 @scottjehl, Filament Group, Inc. Licensed MIT  */
(function( w ){
  // rel=preload support test
  if( !w.loadCSS ){
    return;
  }
  var rp = loadCSS.relpreload = {};
  rp.support = function(){
    try {
      return w.document.createElement( "link" ).relList.supports( "preload" );
    } catch (e) {
      return false;
    }
  };

  // loop preload links and fetch using loadCSS
  rp.poly = function(){
    var links = w.document.getElementsByTagName( "link" );
    for( var i = 0; i < links.length; i++ ){
      var link = links[ i ];
      if( link.rel === "preload" && link.getAttribute( "as" ) === "style" ){
        w.loadCSS( link.href, link );
        link.rel = null;
      }
    }
  };

  // if link[rel=preload] is not supported, we must fetch the CSS manually using loadCSS
  if( !rp.support() ){
    rp.poly();
    var run = w.setInterval( rp.poly, 300 );
    if( w.addEventListener ){
      w.addEventListener( "load", function(){
        w.clearInterval( run );
      } );
    }
    if( w.attachEvent ){
      w.attachEvent( "onload", function(){
        w.clearInterval( run );
      } )
    }
  }
}( this ));

        <div class="overline">Research Article</div>
            <h1 class="article__headline"><div class="highwire-cite-title">The <em>Amborella</em> Genome and the Evolution of Flowering Plants</div></h1>
            <div class="article__expandable-area collapsed">
      <div class="article byline byline--article"><div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup"><div xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" class="contributors"><ol class="contributor-list" id="contrib-group-1"><li class="contributor" id="contrib-1"><span class="collab" id="collab-1"><em>Amborella</em> Genome Project</span><a id="xref-corresp-1-1" class="xref-corresp" href="#corresp-1">*</a><span class="xref-sep">,</span><a id="xref-fn-1-1" class="xref-fn" href="#fn-1">&dagger;</a></li></ol><ol class="corresp-list"><li class="corresp" id="corresp-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-corresp-1-1">&crarr;</a><span class="corresp-label">*</span>Address for general correspondence: <span class="em-link"><span class="em-addr">cwd3{at}psu.edu</span></span>. <div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container article__body">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_sci_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_sci_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup">
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" data-highwire-glossary-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="section structured" id="abstract-1"><h2>Structured Abstract</h2>
<div id="sec-1" class="subsection"><h3>Introduction</h3>

<p id="p-2">Darwin famously characterized the rapid rise and early diversification of flowering plants (angiosperms) in the fossil record as an “abominable mystery.” Identifying genomic changes that accompanied the origin of angiosperms is key to unraveling the molecular basis of biological innovations that contributed to their geologically near-instantaneous rise to ecological dominance.</p>
</div>
<div id="sec-2" class="subsection">
<div class="abstract-figure" id="boxed-text-1"><p id="p-3" class="abstract-graphic"><span class="highwire-responsive-lazyload"><img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" class="highwire-embed lazyload" alt="Embedded Image" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/sites/default/files/highwire/sci/342/6165/1241089/embed/inline-graphic-1.gif"/><noscript><img class="highwire-embed" alt="Embedded Image" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/sites/default/files/highwire/sci/342/6165/1241089/embed/inline-graphic-1.gif"/></noscript></span></p><p id="p-4" class="abstract-caption"><strong><em>Amborella trichopoda</em>, an understory shrub endemic to New Caledonia, is the sole surviving sister species of all other living flowering plants (angiosperms). The <em>Amborella</em> genome provides an exceptional reference for inferring features of the first flowering plants and identifies an ancient angiosperm-wide whole-genome duplication (red star). <em>Amborella</em> flowers have spirally arranged tepals, unfused carpels (female; shown), and laminar stamens.</strong> <em>Amborella trichopoda</em>, an understory shrub endemic to New Caledonia, is the sole surviving sister species of all other living flowering plants (angiosperms). The <em>Amborella</em> genome provides an exceptional reference for inferring features of the first flowering plants and identifies an ancient angiosperm-wide whole-genome duplication (red star). <em>Amborella</em> flowers have spirally arranged tepals, unfused carpels (female; shown), and laminar stamens.</p></div>
</div>
<div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Methods</h3>

<p id="p-5">We provide a draft genome for <em>Amborella trichopoda</em>, the single living representative of the sister lineage to all other extant flowering plants and use phylogenomic and comparative genomic analyses to elucidate ancestral gene content and genome structure in the most recent common ancestor of all living angiosperms.</p>
</div>
<div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Results</h3>

<p id="p-6">We reveal that an ancient genome duplication predated angiosperm diversification. However, unlike all other sequenced angiosperm genomes, the <em>Amborella </em>genome shows no evidence of more recent, lineage-specific genome duplications, making <em>Amborella </em>particularly well suited to help interpret genomic changes after polyploidy in other angiosperms. The remarkable conservation of gene order (synteny) among the genomes of <em>Amborella</em> and other angiosperms has enabled reconstruction of the ancestral gene arrangement in eudicots (~75% of all angiosperms). An ancestral angiosperm gene set was inferred to contain at least 14,000 protein-coding genes; subsequent changes in gene content and genome structure across disparate flowering plant lineages are associated with the evolution of important crops and model species. Relative to nonangiosperm seed plants, 1179 gene lineages first appeared in association with the origin of the angiosperms. These include genes important in flowering, wood formation, and responses to environmental stress. Unlike other angiosperms, the <em>Amborella</em> genome lacks evidence for recent transposon insertions while retaining ancient and divergent transposons. The genome harbors an abundance of atypical lineage-specific 24-nucleotide microRNAs, with at least 27 regulatory microRNA families inferred to have been present in the ancestral angiosperm. Population genomic analysis of 12 individuals from across the small native range of <em>Amborella </em>in New Caledonia reveals geographic structure with conservation implications, as well as both a recent genetic bottleneck and high levels of genome diversity.</p>
</div>
<div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Discussion</h3>

<p id="p-7">The <em>Amborella</em> genome is a pivotal reference for understanding genome and gene family evolution throughout angiosperm history. Genome structure and phylogenomic analyses indicate that the ancestral angiosperm was a polyploid with a large constellation of both novel and ancient genes that survived to play key roles in angiosperm biology.</p>
</div>
</div><div class="section editor-summary" id="abstract-2"><h2>Shaping Plant Evolution</h2><p id="p-8"><em>Amborella trichopoda</em> is understood to be the most basal extant flowering plant and its genome is anticipated to provide insights into the evolution of plant life on Earth (see the Perspective by <strong><a href="/lookup/doi/10.1126/science.1248709">Adams</a></strong>). To validate and assemble the sequence, <strong>Chamala <em>et al.</em></strong> (p. <a href="/lookup/doi/10.1126/science.1241130">1516</a>) combined fluorescent in situ hybridization (FISH), genomic mapping, and next-generation sequencing. The <strong>Amborella Genome Project</strong> (p. <a href="/lookup/doi/10.1126/science.1241089">10.1126/science.1241089</a>) was able to infer that a whole-genome duplication event preceded the evolution of this ancestral angiosperm, and <strong>Rice <em>et al.</em></strong> (p. <a href="/lookup/doi/10.1126/science.1246275">1468</a>) found that numerous genes in the mitochondrion were acquired by horizontal gene transfer from other plants, including almost four entire mitochondrial genomes from mosses and algae.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-4"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p id="p-10"><em>Amborella trichopoda</em> is strongly supported as the single living species of the sister lineage to all other extant flowering plants, providing a unique reference for inferring the genome content and structure of the most recent common ancestor (MRCA) of living angiosperms. Sequencing the <em>Amborella</em> genome, we identified an ancient genome duplication predating angiosperm diversification, without evidence of subsequent, lineage-specific genome duplications. Comparisons between <em>Amborella</em> and other angiosperms facilitated reconstruction of the ancestral angiosperm gene content and gene order in the MRCA of core eudicots. We identify new gene families, gene duplications, and floral protein-protein interactions that first appeared in the ancestral angiosperm. Transposable elements in <em>Amborella</em> are ancient and highly divergent, with no recent transposon radiations. Population genomic analysis across <em>Amborella</em>’s native range in New Caledonia reveals a recent genetic bottleneck and geographic structure with conservation implications.</p>
</div><p id="p-11">The origin of angiosperms (flowering plants) prompted one of Earth’s greatest terrestrial radiations, famously characterized by Charles Darwin as “an abominable mystery” (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1"><em>1</em></a>). The oldest angiosperm fossils date from 130 to 136 million years ago (Ma), but the crown age for the angiosperms has been estimated to be at least 160 Ma (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2"><em>2</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). The origin of the flowering plants was followed by a rapid rise to ecological dominance before the end of the Cretaceous. Angiosperms have since diversified to at least 350,000 species, occupying nearly all terrestrial and many aquatic environments. Angiosperms provide the vast majority of human food and contribute massively to global photosynthesis and carbon sequestration. Understanding angiosperm evolution and diversification is therefore essential to elucidating key processes that underlie the assembly of biotic associations and entire ecosystems.</p><p id="p-12">Paleobotany, phylogenetics, and developmental biology have dramatically reshaped views of the origin and early diversification of angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8"><em>8</em></a>). Most phylogenetic analyses examining chloroplast (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9"><em>9</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12"><em>12</em></a>), large multigene nuclear (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6"><em>6</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13"><em>13</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14"><em>14</em></a>), and chloroplast, mitochondrial, and nuclear genes combined (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15"><em>15</em></a>) strongly support <em>Amborella trichopoda</em>, an understory shrub endemic to New Caledonia, as the single sister species to all other extant angiosperms (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16"><em>16</em></a>). Sister lineages such as <em>Amborella</em>, when compared with other key lineages, can provide unique insights into ancestral characteristics, including genome structure and gene content. Specifically, comparisons of the <em>Amborella</em> genome reported here to other sequenced angiosperm genomes distinguish the genomic features of the most recent common ancestor (MRCA) of all extant flowering plants from those acquired later within individual angiosperm lineages.</p><figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F1">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Amborella is sister to all other extant angiosperms.An overview of land plant phylogeny is shown, including the relationships among major lineages of angiosperms. <div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure><div class="section other1" id="sec-6">
<h2>Genome Assembly and Annotation</h2>
<p id="p-14">The genome of <em>Amborella</em> was sequenced and assembled using a whole-genome shotgun approach that combined more than 23 Gb of single- and paired-end sequence data (~30×) obtained from multiple sequencing platforms (table S1) (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18"><em>18</em></a>). Our assembly comprises 5745 scaffolds totaling 706 Mb, 81% of an earlier genome size estimate of 870 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19"><em>19</em></a>) and 94% of our sequence-based estimate of 748 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-17-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-18-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18"><em>18</em></a>), with a mean scaffold length of 123 kb, an N50 length of 4.9 Mb, and a maximum scaffold length of 16 Mb (table S2). Ninety percent of the assembled genome is contained in 155 scaffolds larger than 1.1 Mb.</p>
<p id="p-15">We evaluated the quality of the assembly using an integrated strategy of comparison with available finished bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) contig sequences (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20"><em>20</em></a>), a BAC-based physical map (<a id="xref-ref-20-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20"><em>20</em></a>), fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH), and whole-genome (optical) mapping (<a id="xref-ref-18-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18"><em>18</em></a>). Accurate and nearly complete coverage of the regions previously characterized through BAC sequencing (<a id="xref-ref-20-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20"><em>20</em></a>) and congruence (99%) with the available physical map verify that the local contig assemblies are of high quality. FISH-based mapping of scaffold ends to chromosomes has thus far confirmed 306 Mb (44%) of the genome assembly (<a id="xref-ref-18-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18"><em>18</em></a>).</p>
<p id="p-16">Annotation of protein-coding genes and repetitive elements was performed with DAWGPAWS (<a id="xref-ref-17-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21"><em>21</em></a>). Despite the different histories of ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD; paleopolyploidy), the number of predicted protein-coding genes in the <em>Amborella</em> genome is similar to the number given in the most recent <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> reference genome annotation (TAIR10, <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org">http://www.arabidopsis.org</a>). Evidence Modeler (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22"><em>22</em></a>) was used to integrate gene annotations, producing 26,846 automated high-confidence gene predictions, 20,301 (76%) of which are supported by transcript evidence. Additionally, 17,089 gene models contain one or more introns, with 86.9% of the splice sites supported by transcript evidence. Refined gene models were further curated through manual comparisons with <em>Amborella</em> complementary DNA transcript assemblies, gene family analyses, and homologous full-length genes from other species (<a id="xref-ref-17-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). Many of the resulting gene models included very long introns relative to other annotated genomes [for example, mean intron length is 1528 bp in <em>Amborella</em>, compared to 165, 966, and 1017 bp in <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>, grape (<em>Vitis vinifera</em>), and Norway spruce (<em>Picea abies</em>), respectively] (<a id="xref-ref-17-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). Annotated high-confidence protein-coding gene models occupied 152 Mb (~21.5% of the genome assembly), including 25.4 Mb of exon sequence. A conservative estimate of 17,095 alternatively spliced protein isoforms was predicted for 6407 intron-containing genes, and multiple splice site variants were inferred for 37.5% of the genes with two or more exons (<a id="xref-ref-17-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>).</p>
<p id="p-17">Gene body methylation is generally conserved in monocots and eudicots (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23"><em>23</em></a>) and has been hypothesized to play an important regulatory role in eukaryotic genomes, distinct from the silencing of transposons (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24"><em>24</em></a>). Whereas gene body methylation is not seen in mosses or lycophytes (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25"><em>25</em></a>), bisulfite sequence mapping indicates that gene body methylation is prevalent in <em>Amborella</em> (fig. S5), suggesting that it is an ancestral feature found in the the MRCA of flowering plants.</p>
</div><div class="section other2" id="sec-7">
<h2>Angiosperm-Wide Genome Duplication</h2>
<p id="p-18">Intragenomic syntenic analysis of <em>Amborella</em> provides clear structural evidence of an ancient WGD event. An <em>Amborella</em> versus <em>Amborella</em> structural comparison shows numerous, duplicate colinear genes (syntenic homeologs) (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2A</a> and fig. S9). Forty-seven intra-<em>Amborella</em> syntenic blocks were identified containing 466 <em>Amborella</em> gene pairs inferred to be descendants of this WGD event (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2A</a> and table S10). Syntenic blocks contain an average of 10 homeologous gene pairs, and the longest block contains 23 gene pairs. Collectively, these 47 blocks include 6565 gene models (out of 26,846), indicating that about one-quarter of the annotated <em>Amborella</em> gene space maps to assembly scaffolds exhibiting synteny-based signal for an ancient WGD event.</p>
<figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F2">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Synteny analysis of Amborella.(A) High-resolution analysis of Amborella-Amborella intragenomic syntenic regions putatively derived from the ancestral angiosperm (epsilon) WGD. Each &lt;em&gt;Amborella&lt;/em&gt; region aligns with up to three regions in grape that resulted from the &lt;em&gt;gamma&lt;/em&gt; hexaploidization event in early core eudicots (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-27-2&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-27&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;27&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;). Syntenic regions within the &lt;em&gt;Amborella&lt;/em&gt; genome were derived from the &lt;em&gt;epsilon&lt;/em&gt; WGD before the origin of all extant angiosperms (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-7-4&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-7&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;7&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;). Similar configurations were obtained for the other six ancestral chromosomes.&lt;/p&gt; &lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F2.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F2-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F2.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 2"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/568455" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F2-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 2</span> 
<span class="caption-title">Synteny analysis of <em>Amborella</em>.</span>
<p id="p-19" class="first-child">(<strong>A</strong>) High-resolution analysis of <em>Amborella-Amborella</em> intragenomic syntenic regions putatively derived from the ancestral angiosperm (<em>epsilon</em>) WGD. Note the series of colinear genes between the two regions. Intragenomic syntenic regions from <em>Amborella</em> are shown when scaffolds are compared and appear as a series of colinear genes between the two regions. (<strong>B</strong>) Macrosynteny and microsynteny between genomic regions in <em>Amborella</em> and grape. Top: Macrosynteny patterns between grape and <em>Amborella</em> and within <em>Amborella</em> scaffolds (only scaffolds 1 to 100 are shown). Each <em>Amborella</em> region aligns with up to three regions in grape that resulted from the <em>gamma</em> hexaploidization event in early core eudicots (<a id="xref-ref-27-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27"><em>27</em></a>). Syntenic regions within the <em>Amborella</em> genome were derived from the <em>epsilon</em> WGD before the origin of all extant angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-7-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). An exemplar set of blocks, showing two homeologous <em>Amborella</em> regions derived from this early WGD, aligns to three distinct grape regions (derived from <em>gamma</em>), with eight parallel regions in total. Bottom: Microsynteny is shown among the eight regions (noted above). Blocks represent genes with orientation on the same strand (blue) or reverse strand (green); shades represent matching gene pairs. (<strong>C</strong>) Gene order alignments between one of the seven hypothesized ancestral core eudicot chromosomes (blue bar), the three post-hexaploidization copies of this chromosome for peach, cacao, and grape chromosomes descending from it (top of figure), and a subset of the <em>Amborella</em> scaffolds (green, bottom of figure). Similar configurations were obtained for the other six ancestral chromosomes.</p>
<div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure>
<p id="p-20">Previous examinations of plant genomes have shown that polyploidy has been a prominent feature in the evolutionary history of angiosperms and that WGD events have had major impacts on genome structure and gene family evolution (<a id="xref-ref-7-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-26-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26"><em>26</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30"><em>30</em></a>). Although most paleopolyploid events detected to date are associated with specific angiosperm families or smaller clades, an older paleohexaploidization (genome triplication), referred to as <em>gamma</em>, has been confirmed in the common ancestor of most eudicots (<a id="xref-ref-26-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26"><em>26</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28"><em>28</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-30-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30"><em>30</em></a>). If the <em>Amborella</em> WGD revealed in this study was an internal, lineage-specific event, a 2:3 syntenic depth ratio would be expected between <em>Amborella</em> and <em>Vitis</em>. Instead, structural analysis shows a clear 1:3 relationship of <em>Amborella</em> and <em>Vitis</em> syntenic blocks that map to the <em>gamma</em> paleopolyploidy (<a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2B</a> and figs. S6 to S8), indicating that the WGD detected in <em>Amborella</em> is not lineage-specific and likely occurred in an ancient common ancestor of the two species, thereby confirming that the divergence of <em>Amborella</em> predates <em>gamma</em> (<a id="xref-ref-20-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20"><em>20</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-26-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26"><em>26</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-27-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27"><em>27</em></a>).</p>
<p id="p-21">Phylogenomic analyses of 11,519 gene families confirm that dispersed, duplicated genes specific to <em>Amborella</em> are uncommon (282 nontandem gene pairs), especially when compared to older gene family expansions shared across angiosperm or seed plant lineages (473 orthogroups with at least 50% bootstrap support) (<a id="xref-ref-17-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). The age distribution of the pre-angiosperm gene duplications is bimodal (fig. S17), with the two peaks corresponding to the same ancestral angiosperm (<em>epsilon</em>) and ancestral seed plant (<em>zeta</em>) genome duplications inferred in previous analyses based on transcriptome data (<a id="xref-ref-7-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). <em>Zeta</em> has escaped syntenic detection in this and other studies of angiosperm synteny, presumably because of extreme gene loss and rearrangements that have accumulated since this hypothesized ancient event more than 300 Ma. To confirm further that the syntenic, duplicated blocks correspond to the same angiosperm-wide duplications discovered through phylogenomics, we manually curated six large duplicated blocks (<a id="xref-fig-2-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2B</a> and fig. S10). Phylogenetic analysis of 155 syntenic gene pairs from these large blocks supports the placement of the <em>epsilon</em> genome duplication on the branch leading to the MRCA of extant angiosperms (77 of 155 gene trees resolved <em>epsilon</em> with bootstrap values of 80% or greater; see table S11).</p>
<p id="p-22">In summary, <em>Amborella</em> genome structure demonstrates no evidence of WGD since this lineage diverged from the rest of the angiosperms at least 160 Ma. However, analyses indicate that paralagous gene copies associated with the <em>epsilon</em> WGD resulted from duplication shortly before the diversification of all living angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-7-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7"><em>7</em></a>). This event represents the most ancient WGD known in plants for which structural evidence persists. The <em>Amborella</em> genome therefore provides a unique evolutionary reference for elucidating genome content and structure in the MRCA of extant angiosperms and for resolving the timing of WGDs and single-gene losses and gains that have contributed to the diversification of the angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-8-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8"><em>8</em></a>).</p>
</div><div class="section other3" id="sec-8">
<h2>Ancestral Gene Order in Core Eudicots</h2>
<p id="p-23">We combined scaffold-level information from <em>Amborella</em> with chromosome-level data from the eudicot rosid lineages of grape (<em>V. vinifera</em>), peach (<em>Prunus persica</em>), and cacao (<em>Theobroma cacao</em>) to reconstruct the hypothetical structure of seven inferred pre-hexaploidization chromosomes in the ancestor of the core eudicots. These three species were chosen because they have retained structurally similar genomes and clear patterns of paralogy among syntenic gene copies (fig. S11), enabling us to assign most genes to one of seven groups of three homeologous chromosomes or segments (<a id="xref-ref-26-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26"><em>26</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-27-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27"><em>27</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-30-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30"><em>30</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31"><em>31</em></a>). A comprehensive analysis of <em>Amborella</em> and the three subgenomes from the representative rosids (combining a number of computational techniques) (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29"><em>29</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-31-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31"><em>31</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32"><em>32</em></a>) enabled a completely automated reconstruction of ancestral gene order beyond the level of “contiguous ancestral regions” [compare (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33"><em>33</em></a>)]. <a id="xref-fig-2-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figure 2C</a> shows the orthologous gene alignments between one of the ancestral chromosomes, an <em>Amborella</em> genome scaffold, and triplicated blocks of genes in the rosid genomes. This analysis, which uses <em>Amborella</em> as an outgroup to the three eudicot genomes, would not have been possible without <em>Amborella</em> or another (as yet undiscovered) non-eudicot genome that retains a large amount of syntenic signal. The prevalence of WGDs in monocots and other basal angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-34-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34"><em>34</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-37-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37"><em>37</em></a>) limits the possibility of identifying such genomes. Similar patterns for all seven ancestral core eudicot chromosomes (<a id="xref-ref-17-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>) illustrate the utility of the <em>Amborella</em> genome for reconstructing ancestral genomes within the angiosperms, thus clarifying the divergence of subgenomes after WGD events. In the case of the reconstructed core eudicot ancestor, tracking the syntenically retained descendant blocks in the three rosids reveals a consistent pattern of subgenome dominance (fig. S15). This pattern, which governs the fractionation likelihood of gene triplets generated by the <em>gamma</em> event, is not evident from the direct comparison of the extant genomes alone and highlights the value of ancestral genome reconstructions enabled by <em>Amborella</em>.</p>
</div><div class="section other4" id="sec-9">
<h2>Ancestral Angiosperm Gene Content</h2>
<p id="p-24">To assess the origin and history of angiosperm genes using <em>Amborella</em> genes as an anchor, we clustered protein-coding genes from 22 sequenced land plant genomes selected for their phylogenetic representation into 53,136 orthogroup clusters (narrowly defined gene lineages; table S12), with annotations provided by the associated pfam domain and full Gene Ontology (GO) terms for genes contained in these clusters (table S16). We further merged the orthogroups into 6054 super-orthogroup clusters representing more inclusively circumscribed gene families (<a id="xref-ref-17-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). The broader circumscription of super-orthogroups allows for the clustering of more divergent homologs, thus increasing the likelihood that they represent truly distinct gene families. Phylogenetic analyses of super-orthogroups can help to root orthogroup phylogenies and resolve the relationships among related orthogroups.</p>
<p id="p-25">We estimated the ancestral gene content at key nodes in land plant phylogeny and modeled the changes of orthogroups occurring along each branch (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a> and tables S13 to S15). The largest changes in gene family content appear to have occurred evolutionarily recently along terminal branches, or are shared among closely related taxa, such as within the tomato (Solanaceae) or crucifer (Brassicaceae) families. Large numbers of orthogroup gains were also inferred along the deeper branches leading to all angiosperms (3285 new orthogroups using parsimony reconstruction) and to grasses (4281 new orthogroups) (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a> and tables S13 to S15). However, because this analysis does not include genome sequences from ferns and gymnosperms, it cannot distinguish between orthogroups originating with euphyllophytes (ferns plus seed plants), seed plants, or angiosperms; consequently, all of these orthogroups are reconstructed along the stem branch leading to angiosperms. We sorted the inferred gene set of the recently published Norway spruce genome (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38"><em>38</em></a>), plus gymnosperm and basal angiosperm transcript assemblies, into this gene classification, and manually reevaluated the origin of orthogroups around the MRCAs of seed plants and angiosperms, thereby resolving or refining the origin of 5210 orthogroups, 1179 (23%) of which are specific to angiosperms or have diverged sufficiently such that none of the gymnosperm homologs were detected, with 4031 (77%) present in the MRCA of seed plants (table S13).</p>
<figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F3">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Ancestral reconstruction of gene family content in land plants.Orthogroup gains and losses are inferred from the global gene family classification of proteins from sequenced plant genomes using a Wagner parsimony framework (17). Triangles are proportional to the number of orthogroup gains (green) and losses (orange). Triangles are proportional to the number of orthogroup gains (green) and losses (orange). Actual values for the gains and losses in this analysis are provided in table S14; an analogous likelihood-based analysis is provided in table S15.&lt;/p&gt; &lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F3.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F3-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F3.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 3"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/568466" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F3-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 3</span> 
<span class="caption-title">Ancestral reconstruction of gene family content in land plants.</span>
<p id="p-26" class="first-child">Orthogroup gains and losses are inferred from the global gene family classification of proteins from sequenced plant genomes using a Wagner parsimony framework (<a id="xref-ref-17-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). Triangles are proportional to the number of orthogroup gains (green) and losses (orange). Actual values for the gains and losses in this analysis are provided in table S14; an analogous likelihood-based analysis is provided in table S15.</p>
<div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure>
<p id="p-27">The large number of orthogroups first appearing in angiosperms suggests that a diverse collection of novel gene functions was likely associated with the origin of flowering plants. Analyses of GO annotations for genes in angiosperm-derived orthogroups revealed the origin of orthogroups with functions associated with key innovations defining the flowering plant clade (table S16) (<a id="xref-ref-17-11" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). GO annotations related to reproduction (flower development, reproductive developmental process, pollination, and similar terms), including MADS-box gene lineages (see below), were overrepresented in this set of orthogroups. Genes with roles in <em>Arabidopsis</em> floral development (table S17) are included in 201 orthogroups, 18 of which were evolutionarily derived in the MRCA of angiosperms. Significant enrichments were also observed for several classes of regulatory genes (transcription, regulation of gene expression and of cellular, biochemical, and metabolic processes) as well as genes involved in various developmental processes. These include genes involved in carpel development (<em>CRABS CLAW</em>), endosperm development (<em>AGL62</em>), stem cell maintenance in meristems (<em>WUSCHEL</em>), and flowering time (<em>FRIGIDA</em>), suggesting that they might be key components underlying the origin of the flower.</p>
<p id="p-28">Once a functional flower evolved, genetic innovations related to reproductive biology continued. Indeed, many gene lineages with genes inferred to have specific stamen (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39"><em>39</em></a>), carpel (<a id="xref-ref-39-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39"><em>39</em></a>), and ovule (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40"><em>40</em></a>) functions apparently arose after the origin of angiosperms, within evolutionarily derived angiosperm lineages (table S18).</p>
<p id="p-29">Whereas the origin of the flower may be partly explained by novel gene lineages that first appeared with the origin of the angiosperms, other floral genes, including putative B-class (that is, petal- and stamen-specific) gene targets (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41"><em>41</em></a>), predate the origin of angiosperms. More than 70% of the gene lineages with known roles in flowering, including genes involved in floral timing and initiation (<em>CO</em>, <em>SOC1</em>, <em>VIN3</em>, <em>VEL1</em>), meristem identity (<em>ULT1</em>, <em>TFL2</em>), and floral structure (<em>AFO</em>, <em>AP2</em>, <em>ETT</em>, <em>HUA2</em>, <em>HEN4</em>, <em>KAN</em>, <em>RPL</em>, <em>JAG</em>), were present in the MRCA of all extant seed plants (table S16) (<a id="xref-ref-17-12" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). Orthogroups for other major components of the floral regulatory pathway are older still, with core components of the pathway present in the ancestral vascular plant (for example, <em>LFY</em>, phytochromes, <em>CLV</em>, <em>SKP1</em>, <em>GA1</em>, <em>SEU</em>, <em>HEN1</em>, and <em>FVE</em>).</p>
<p id="p-30">Together, these observations suggest that orthologs of most floral genes existed long before their specific roles were established in flowering, and that they were later co-opted to serve floral functions. After the origin of angiosperms, new genes originated or were recruited to refine or more narrowly parse functions associated with flower development. This pattern is consistent with the observation that the floral organ transcriptional program is canalized (entrained) in eudicots relative to the less organ-constrained transcriptomes of earlier-diverging, less species-diverse angiosperm lineages (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42"><em>42</em></a>).</p>
<p id="p-31">Many of the novel gene lineages that first arose in angiosperms play no specific role in reproductive processes. Orthogroups containing genes with specific functions in vessel formation (<em>VND7</em> and <em>NAC083</em>) also first appeared at this time, even though <em>Amborella</em> does not produce vessels, but only tracheids (see below). Perhaps surprisingly, the most highly enriched GO terms in orthogroups derived in angiosperms were associated with homeostatic processes (GO:0042592; 18.9-fold enrichment). Relevant to the importance of plant-herbivore coevolution in the diversification of angiosperms and insects (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43"><em>43</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44"><em>44</em></a>), the next most highly enriched GO classification was for genes involved in response to external stimuli (GO:0009605; 10.9-fold enrichment), including those with expression elicited by herbivory.</p>
<p id="p-32">Enrichment patterns for functional categories were similar in the ancestral seed plant and ancestral angiosperm (table S16), including novel lineages of genes involved in reproductive, regulatory, and developmental processes. GO classifications associated with pollen-pistil interaction and epigenetic modification were enriched in orthogroups arising on the branch leading to seed plants, but not in the lineage leading to the ancestral angiosperm (table S16), the former perhaps indicating that some angiosperm-specific reproductive features predated angiospermous (enclosed ovule) reproduction.</p>
</div><div class="section other5" id="sec-10">
<h2>Gene Family Expansions in Angiosperms</h2>
<p id="p-33">Expansions of many gene families are evident in <em>Amborella</em>, and phylogenetic analyses indicate that such expansions occurred in the ancestral angiosperm, accompanying innovations associated with angiosperm origin. Using <em>Amborella</em> as a reference, we examined patterns of gene family diversification in angiosperm evolution, often in association with phenotypic divergence among angiosperm lineages.</p>
<div id="sec-11" class="subsection">
<h3><em>MADS-Box Genes</em></h3>
<p id="p-34">MADS-box transcription factors are among the most important regulators of flower development. The <em>Amborella</em> genome encodes 36 MADS-box genes (table S19) (<a id="xref-ref-17-13" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>), fewer than in other angiosperms (for example, <em>Arabidopsis</em> and rice), but consistent with the lack of a lineage-specific WGD. These genes belong to 21 clades, each of which includes genes from at least one other major lineage of angiosperms, implying that a minimum set of 21 MADS-box genes existed in the MRCA of extant angiosperms (figs. S19 and S20). The <em>Amborella</em> genome reveals that floral organ identity genes from eight major lineages (that is, <em>AP1</em>/<em>SQUA</em>, <em>AP3</em>/<em>DEF</em>, <em>PI</em>/<em>GLO</em>, <em>AG</em>, <em>STK</em>, <em>AGL2</em>/<em>SEP1</em>, <em>AGL9</em>/<em>SEP3</em>, and <em>AGL6</em>; <a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4A</a>) existed in the MRCA of extant angiosperms and were likely derived from three ancestral lineages in the MRCA of extant seed plants. These data support the hypothesis that duplication and diversification of floral MADS-box genes likely occurred before the origin of extant angiosperms, despite being tightly associated with the origin of the flower. Furthermore, the previously presumed monocot-specific <em>OsMADS32</em> and eudicot-specific <em>TM8</em> gene lineages (fig. S20) (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45"><em>45</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47"><em>47</em></a>) have orthologs in <em>Amborella</em>, suggesting that they were likely present in the earliest angiosperms and were subsequently lost in eudicots or monocots, respectively.</p>
<figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F4">
  <div class="figure__head highwire-figure">
    <div class="fig-inline"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Amborella as the reference for understanding the molecular developmental genetics of flower evolution.(A) A schematic diagram showing the evolutionary history of floral MADS-box genes. Note that all of the eight major gene lineages existed in the MRCA of extant angiosperms. (B) Evolutionary changes in the ability of B-class MADS-box proteins to form homodimers and heterodimers. In gymnosperms, the proteins of B-class genes can only form homodimers or semi-homodimers (that is, heterodimers formed by products of recently duplicated genes), whereas in the MRCA of extant angiosperms, they gained the ability to form heterodimers between members of different lineages. The interrupted lines represent previously described gene loss events (85, 86)." class="fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-940096486" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;fig-label&quot;&gt;Fig. 4&lt;/span&gt;  &lt;span class=&quot;caption-title&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;Amborella&lt;/em&gt; as the reference for understanding the molecular developmental genetics of flower evolution.&lt;/span&gt; &lt;p id=&quot;p-35&quot; class=&quot;first-child&quot;&gt;(&lt;strong&gt;A&lt;/strong&gt;) A schematic diagram showing the evolutionary history of floral MADS-box genes. Note that all of the eight major gene lineages existed in the MRCA of extant angiosperms. (&lt;strong&gt;B&lt;/strong&gt;) Evolutionary changes in the ability of B-class MADS-box proteins to form homodimers and heterodimers. In gymnosperms, the proteins of B-class genes can only form homodimers or semi-homodimers (that is, heterodimers formed by products of recently duplicated genes), whereas in the MRCA of extant angiosperms, they gained the ability to form heterodimers between members of different lineages. The interrupted lines represent previously described gene loss events (&lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-85-1&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-85&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;85&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a id=&quot;xref-ref-86-1&quot; class=&quot;xref-bibr&quot; href=&quot;#ref-86&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;86&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;).&lt;/p&gt; &lt;div class=&quot;sb-div caption-clear&quot;/&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="fragment-image lazyload" aria-describedby="F4-caption" src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F4.medium.gif"/><noscript><img class="fragment-image" aria-describedby="F4-caption" src="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F4.medium.gif"/></noscript></span></a></div>        <div class="figure__options">
      <ul class="highwire-figure-links"><li class="0 first"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download link-icon" title="Download Fig. 4"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download high-res image</span></a></li>
<li class="1"><a href="https://science.sciencemag.org/content/sci/342/6165/1241089/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab link-icon" target="_blank"><i class="fa fa-external-link"></i> <span class="title">Open in new tab</span></a></li>
<li class="2 last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/568473" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt link-icon"><i class="fa fa-download"></i> <span class="title">Download Powerpoint</span></a></li>
</ul>    </div>
      </div>
    <figcaption class="fig-caption" id="F4-caption">
    <span class="fig-label">Fig. 4</span> 
<span class="caption-title"><em>Amborella</em> as the reference for understanding the molecular developmental genetics of flower evolution.</span>
<p id="p-35" class="first-child">(<strong>A</strong>) A schematic diagram showing the evolutionary history of floral MADS-box genes. Note that all of the eight major gene lineages existed in the MRCA of extant angiosperms. (<strong>B</strong>) Evolutionary changes in the ability of B-class MADS-box proteins to form homodimers and heterodimers. In gymnosperms, the proteins of B-class genes can only form homodimers or semi-homodimers (that is, heterodimers formed by products of recently duplicated genes), whereas in the MRCA of extant angiosperms, they gained the ability to form heterodimers between members of different lineages. The interrupted lines represent previously described gene loss events (<a id="xref-ref-85-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85"><em>85</em></a>, <a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86"><em>86</em></a>).</p>
<div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div>  </figcaption>
  </figure>
<p id="p-36">MADS-box transcription factors in floral development form dimers or higher-level complexes that bind to their targets with more complex patterns than those in gymnosperms (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48"><em>48</em></a>). We conducted a comprehensive series of yeast two-hybrid assays among the <em>Amborella</em> floral MADS-box transcription factors. The protein-protein interaction (PPI) patterns in <em>Amborella</em> (fig. S21) are generally consistent with those in other angiosperms, and show clear differences from those in gymnosperms. For example, the B-function <em>AP3</em>/<em>DEF</em> and <em>PI</em>/<em>GLO</em> genes represent duplicate lineages in early angiosperms, arising after the divergence from the gymnosperms. The <em>Amborella</em> AP3 and PI homologs form heterodimers, as in other angiosperms, whereas the single AP3/PI homologs in gymnosperms form only homodimers, with heterodimers only occurring between recent duplicates of the AP3/PI homologs (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4B</a>). B function is essential for the development of petals and other petal-like organs, which represent one of the most prominent novel floral features and exhibit extraordinary diversity in form; therefore, evolutionary shifts in PPI patterns after gene duplications, along with changes in gene sequence and expression patterns, likely have been crucial for functional innovations in the regulatory network for reproductive organ development and the origin of the flower (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49"><em>49</em></a>), as well as for functional diversification of the many floral forms among lineages of angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50"><em>50</em></a>).</p>
</div>
<div id="sec-12" class="subsection">
<h3><em>Glycogen Synthase Kinase 3 (GSK3) genes</em></h3>
<p id="p-37"><em>GSK3</em> genes encode signal transduction proteins with roles in a variety of biological processes in eukaryotes. In contrast to their low copy numbers in animals, <em>GSK3</em> genes are numerous in land plants and have diverse functions, including floral development in angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51"><em>51</em></a>). Five <em>GSK3</em> loci that were present in the ancestral angiosperm have subsequently diversified among major angiosperm lineages, but a sixth ancestral locus has been detected only in <em>Amborella</em> (fig. S22). Thus, among flowering plants, <em>Amborella</em> alone may contain all the <em>GSK3</em> gene lineages that arose before the origin of extant angiosperms, underscoring the importance of <em>Amborella</em> for reconstructing the ancestral angiosperm genome (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52"><em>52</em></a>).</p>
</div>
<div id="sec-13" class="subsection">
<h3><em>Seed Storage Globulins</em></h3>
<p id="p-38">Seed storage proteins, including globulins, are critical for embryo and early seedling development in seed plants. These proteins are embedded in the very diverse cupin superfamily, which is distributed across the tree of life (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53"><em>53</em></a>). The 11S legumin-type globulins are widespread across the seed plant phylogeny [for example, (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54"><em>54</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56"><em>56</em></a>)]. Three distinct 11S legumin-type globulins have been identified in proteomic analyses of the globulin fraction in <em>Amborella</em> seeds (table S21) (<a id="xref-ref-17-14" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). Comparisons of the <em>Amborella</em> globulin-coding gene sequences to other seed plants revealed that key cysteine residues contributing to disulfide bonding between subunits and the absence of Intron IV, found in gymnosperms, are conserved characteristics of angiosperm legumins (fig. S25). In contrast, a conserved 52-residue region present in soybean, and thought to be important for mature hexamer formation (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57"><em>57</em></a>), was apparently derived after the divergence of <em>Amborella</em> from other angiosperms (fig. S26). Globally, both structural (fig. S26) and phylogenetic (fig. S27 and table S22) analyses support the view that <em>Amborella</em> 11S globulins can both be reminiscent of those in monocots and eudicots and exhibit specific features of corresponding seed storage proteins in basal angiosperms and gymnosperms.</p>
</div>
<div id="sec-14" class="subsection">
<h3><em>Terpene Synthase Genes</em></h3>
<p id="p-39">Terpenoids constitute the largest class of plant secondary metabolites and play important roles in plant ecological interactions (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58"><em>58</em></a>). Biosynthesis of plant terpenoids is driven by terpene synthases (TPS). The <em>Amborella</em> TPS family contains more than 30 members, comparable in size to those of other angiosperms. However, the sesquiterpene synthase subfamily a (TPS-a), which is present in dicots, monocots, and Magnoliaceae but absent in gymnosperms and nonseed plants (<a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59"><em>59</em></a>), is also absent in <em>Amborella</em> (fig. S28) (<a id="xref-ref-17-15" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). This indicates that the occurrence and diversification of this subfamily likely happened after the divergence of <em>Amborella</em> from other angiosperms, although its presence or absence in other basal angiosperms still needs to be established. Sesquiterpene synthases are involved in the production of C15 terpenoids, which are involved in diverse biological processes including the production of floral scents used to attract pollinators. <em>Amborella</em> lacks any detectable floral volatiles (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60"><em>60</em></a>), and the expansion of the TPS-a subfamily may therefore have played an important role in the subsequent radiation of flowering plants.</p>
</div>
<div id="sec-15" class="subsection">
<h3><em>Cell Wall and Lignin Genes</em></h3>
<p id="p-40">Secondary cell walls of woody plants contain lignin (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61"><em>61</em></a>), facilitating water transport and mechanical support in xylem (<a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62"><em>62</em></a>). Most gymnosperms (cycads, <em>Ginkgo</em>, and conifers) have a predominant guaiacyl (G) subunit type of lignin, whereas the gnetophytes (&lt;100 species) and the woody angiosperms have a lignin characterized by a copolymer of syringyl S and G subunits (S/G lignin). S/G lignin has also been found in the lycophyte <em>Selaginella moellendorffii</em>, suggesting that S/G lignin may have evolved more than once in plant evolution (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63"><em>63</em></a>). S/G lignin is associated with cells involved in mechanical support, whereas G-type lignin has been associated with water transport (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64"><em>64</em></a>). <em>Amborella</em> produces an S/G type of lignin, but the relative proportion of S subunits is much lower (13%) than values typical of woody angiosperms (50 to 70%) (tables S24 to S26). The low S/G ratio of <em>Amborella</em> might represent an ancestral condition that was transitional between gymnosperms and other angiosperms. However, the underlying genes of lignin precursor biosynthesis in <em>Amborella</em> are typical of woody angiosperms (table S27 and fig. S29, A to H). Although <em>Amborella</em> lacks vessels, in contrast to nearly all other angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65"><em>65</em></a>), the wood cell walls of <em>Amborella</em> are xylan-rich (table S24), typical of angiosperms (<a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66"><em>66</em></a>). <em>Amborella</em> contains all of the carbohydrate-active enzyme families found in angiosperms (table S28) (<a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67"><em>67</em></a>), but lacks many of the more derived clades of genes seen in other angiosperms. Indeed, much of the diversity of cell wall genes in angiosperms (for example, glycosyltransferase family 37; fig. S25) appears to result from gene duplication after the divergence of <em>Amborella</em> from other angiosperms.</p>
</div>
</div><div class="section other6" id="sec-16">
<h2>Transposable Element Content in <em>Amborella</em></h2>
<p id="p-41">As in the Norway spruce genome (<a id="xref-ref-38-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38"><em>38</em></a>), the average age of identifiable transposable elements (TEs) in <em>Amborella</em> is considerably older than that of other angiosperm genomes [for example, (<a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68"><em>68</em></a>–<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70"><em>70</em></a>)]. Likewise, ancient, full-length long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons were identifiable in <em>Amborella</em> more than an estimated 40 million years after insertion (<a id="xref-ref-17-16" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). Wicker <em>et al.</em> (<a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71"><em>71</em></a>) established the convention of separating LTR retrotransposons exhibiting more than 80% divergence in their terminal repeats into distinct families, but nearly 10% of individual <em>Amborella</em> LTR elements show a greater degree of divergence between their terminal repeats. Therefore, we used a clustering approach to circumscribe TE families. Median estimated insertion times for LTR subfamilies with two or more detectable TEs ranged from 4.0 to 17.6 Ma. A large class of <em>Gypsy</em> LTR retrotransposons with 502 annotated TEs experienced the most recent burst of activity 0.5 Ma (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-17-17" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17"><em>17</em></a>). Endogenous pararetroviruses (EPRVs) were a relatively large component of the repeat landscape, comprising 2.4% of the assembled <em>Amborella</em> genome. Similar to <em>Sorghum bicolor</em>, which has a comparable genome size, TEs and EPRVs account for 57.2% of the nonambiguous nucleotides in the <em>Amborella</em> genome (668 Mb, table S30), but TE insertion times estimated for the <em>Amborella</em> genome are much older than inferred for <em>Sorghum</em> (<a id="xref-ref-64-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64"><em>64</em></a>). Only four of the common superclasses of DNA TEs were observed (table S30); CACTA and TC1/Mariner-type elements were not detected. Most DNA TEs were highly degraded, with highly divergent sequences and missing terminal inverted repeats, again suggesting the persistence of identifiable elements over millions of years. The lack of recent transposon activity in the <em>Amborella</em> genome may be due to very effective silencing or the loss of active transposases.</p>
<figure class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd figure figure--data" id="F5">
